Thank you, gm</pre><strong>Commenter</strong>: Anthony O'Dowd - a_o-dowd@uk.ibm.com<br><strong>CommentLine</strong>: [docs/source/ledger.md#L7](https://github.com/hyperledger-gerrit-archive/fabric/blob/bcf333f9fa4328702b3cbf5b54cd5e7fa16c15e1/docs/source/ledger.md#L7)<br><strong>Comment</strong>: <pre>Hi Greg, Happy to address this comment when we do the change. Lots of comments from Sjir and team in this space too!</pre><strong>Commenter</strong>: Anthony O'Dowd - a_o-dowd@uk.ibm.com<br><strong>CommentLine</strong>: [docs/source/ledger.md#L7](https://github.com/hyperledger-gerrit-archive/fabric/blob/bcf333f9fa4328702b3cbf5b54cd5e7fa16c15e1/docs/source/ledger.md#L7)<br><strong>Comment</strong>: <pre>Hi Greg,  we restructured the intro para to make this very clear I hope.  You'll see the ledger explained in terms of its two components, one that has this immutable quality, and one that maintains the current state. 

I've added a specific para to address your needs Greg -- see what you think./  Happy to address any follow-ups.

Every transaction committed to the blockchain records a create, read, update or
delete operation to the world state. Whereas the blockchain can only append
transactions, the world state database is kept up-to-date by applying the
blockchain transaction operations of create, read, update and delete. In a
nutshell, it's the ledger history that is immutable -- the ledger state changes
all the time!</pre></blockquote><h3>PatchSet Number: 2</h3><blockquote><strong>Type</strong>: REWORK<br><strong>Author</strong>: Anthony O'Dowd - a_o-dowd@uk.ibm.com<br><strong>Uploader</strong>: Anthony O'Dowd - The sequence *can* be changed. It's the individual blocks that don't change. If this word was chosen in an effort to not use the word "immutable", I'm all for it. But I think we should find a way to say this in a different way. 

In fact, I'd probably put off the whole notion of immutability to a second paragraph. First, we establish the notion of an electronic ledger and what it represents (ie, the accumulation of all the updates to the ledger, of which there are different types). That's the first thing. Then I think we move on to immutability and what that means.</pre><strong>Commenter</strong>: Joe Alewine - Joe.Alewine@ibm.com<br><strong>CommentLine</strong>: [docs/source/ledger/ledger.md#L4](https://github.com/hyperledger-gerrit-archive/fabric/blob/bd3d93180b35eb7470b63c06c093533791aba850/docs/source/ledger/ledger.md#L4)<br><strong>Comment</strong>: <pre>I should have added that what I meant is that the sequence can be changed *by adding to it*. a_o-dowd@uk.ibm.com<br><strong>CommentLine</strong>: [docs/source/ledger/ledger.md#L22](https://github.com/hyperledger-gerrit-archive/fabric/blob/bd3d93180b35eb7470b63c06c093533791aba850/docs/source/ledger/ledger.md#L22)<br><strong>Comment</strong>: <pre>> is this correct? ledger on each peer is kept consistent through "consensus"...i thought ordering of transactions is done through "consensus"

Consensus is the fact that everyone agrees that a change can be made -- it refers to fact that everyone who's needed to agree the decision, agrees to it -- and thus the ledgers being kept consistent.  The orderer is how this happens, not what it is. 1/It's a book 
2/It has pages
3/Each page has a list of transactions
4/At the top and bottom of each page  there's a set of totals that show the position at the beginning of the page, and at the end.
5/Hyperledger Fabric is a ledger like this - it has both transactions held in blocks, analogous to the pages, and world state, which is like the totals at the bottom of each page (but a database, not just a set of numbers)

Written well, this can really help the general reader.</pre><strong>Commenter</strong>: Anthony O'Dowd - a_o-dowd@uk.ibm.com<br><strong>CommentLine</strong>: [docs/source/ledger/ledger.md#L2](https://github.com/hyperledger-gerrit-archive/fabric/blob/24e020436b03d7a2c43c27878ceb4a860e179888/docs/source/ledger/ledger.md#L2)<br><strong>Comment</strong>: <pre>Done</pre><strong>Commenter</strong>: Anthony O'Dowd - The Block header consists of three fields, the block number, an integer starting with 0 (genesys block) and always increased by 1 for the block to be appended, the hash of he previous block and the hash of the current block. These 3 fields are written by the ordering system.  The Block data section contains as main elements the proposed transaction, the Read-Write sets, the certificate, public key and signature of each endorser, This section is written by the ordering system. The third section, the Block Metadata contains the certificate, public key and signature of the orderer writing the block, written by the ordering system as well as an array having for each transaction the value valid or invalid, written by the committer.</pre><strong>Commenter</strong>: Chris Gabriel - We've added too much detail -- channels, peers, ordering service.  NONE of this is important right now.  We're trying to talk about the WHAT of the ledger, not the HOW of it's implementation.  

I'd like to simplify this as follows: 

It's helpful to think of there being one **logical** ledger for each channel in	
the network. In reality, each node in the network maintains its own copy of a
ledger -- which is kept consistent with every other copy through a
process called **consensus**. The term **Distributed Ledger Technology**
(**DLT**) is often associated with this kind of ledger -- one that is logically
singular, but has many consistent copies distributed throughout a network.</pre><strong>Commenter</strong>: Anthony O'Dowd - a_o-dowd@uk.ibm.com<br><strong>CommentLine</strong>: [docs/source/ledger/ledger.md#L51](https://github.com/hyperledger-gerrit-archive/fabric/blob/73622de3688c676faa65c915d0a7a0e6f8ed9797/docs/source/ledger/ledger.md#L51)<br><strong>Comment</strong>: <pre>Done</pre><strong>Commenter</strong>: Sjir Nijssen - With Fabric you cannot go to a certain prior point in time ('page' or block) and know the overall state at that time. You have to play all prior transactions to know the state at that time. I think it would be sufficient to say that the ledger is a journal of business transactions.
